  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_d672a6c4-7c1b-4554-9826-8d5a2bbf12de'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer11444171115457485028">A long-awaited collection of stories--twelve in all--by one of the most exciting writers at work today, the acclaimed author of <i>Who Will Run the Frog Hospital?</i> and <i>Self-Help.</i> Stories remarkable in their range, emotional force, and dark laughter, and in the sheer beauty and power of their language.<br/><br/>From the opening story, "Willing", about a second-rate movie actress in her t</span>
  <span id="freeText11444171115457485028" style="display:none">A long-awaited collection of stories--twelve in all--by one of the most exciting writers at work today, the acclaimed author of <i>Who Will Run the Frog Hospital?</i> and <i>Self-Help.</i> Stories remarkable in their range, emotional force, and dark laughter, and in the sheer beauty and power of their language.<br /><br />From the opening story, "Willing", about a second-rate movie actress in her thirties who has moved back to Chicago, where she makes a seedy motel room her home and becomes involved with a mechanic who has not the least idea of who she is as a human being, <i>Birds of America</i> unfolds a startlingly brilliant series of portraits of the unhinged, the lost, the unsettled of our America. <br /><br />In the story "Which Is More Than I Can Say About Some People" ("There is nothing as complex in the world--no flower or stone--as a single hello from a human being"), a woman newly separated from her husband is on a long-planned trip through Ireland with her mother. When they set out on an expedition to kiss the Blarney Stone, the image of wisdom and success that her mother has always put forth slips away to reveal the panicky woman she really is. <br /><br />In "Charades," a family game at Christmas is transformed into a hilarious and insightful (and fundamentally upsetting) revelation of crumbling family ties. <br /><br />In "Community Life,"a shy, almost reclusive, librarian, Transylvania-born and Vermont-bred, moves in with her boyfriend, the local anarchist in a small university town, and all hell breaks loose. And in "Four Calling Birds, Three French Hens," a woman who goes through the stages of grief as she mourns the death of her cat (Anger, Denial, Bargaining, Haagen Dazs, Rage) is seen by her friends as really mourning other issues: the impending death of her parents, the son she never had, Bosnia.<br /><br />In what may be her most stunning book yet, Lorrie Moore explores the personal and the universal, the idiosyncratic and the mundane, with all the wit, brio, and verve that have made her one of the best storytellers of our time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11444171115457485028" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1218067978" />
    <a title="Barbara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2172096-barbara"><img alt="Barbara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598060221p2/2172096.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1218067978?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barbara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barbara" href="/user/show/2172096-barbara">Barbara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1218067978" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6407405473658490566"><br/>I don't usually read short story collections but picked up this one for a book club.<br/><br/>Moore's stories are well-written and insightful, and she can write humorous scenes, but overall I thought the book was somewhat depressing. In the end I was left with the impression that it's almost impossible for two people to have a fulfilling relationship and almost everyone is unhappy in one way or another. Though this may very well be true it's still dispiriting to read about.<br/><br/>In some of these stories charact</span>
  <span id="freeText6407405473658490566" style="display:none"><br>I don't usually read short story collections but picked up this one for a book club.<br><br>Moore's stories are well-written and insightful, and she can write humorous scenes, but overall I thought the book was somewhat depressing. In the end I was left with the impression that it's almost impossible for two people to have a fulfilling relationship and almost everyone is unhappy in one way or another. Though this may very well be true it's still dispiriting to read about.<br><br>In some of these stories characters hook up with the wrong people because they're lonely and needy - and then are disappointed. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1598978407i/30046892.png" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In other stories characters have dishonest and/or unfaithful partners and have a hard time dealing with it (throw the bum out would be my view - but this may be easier said than done).<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1598978407i/30046893.png" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>One story is about parents coping with a baby who's stricken with cancer. Another is about a woman who was holding a friend's baby when an accident occurs, killing the child; of course the woman blames herself and can hardly go on.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1598978407i/30046894.png" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I think my favorite story is about an unhappy spouse who learns to use a gun, then gets to shoot a nutcase who breaks into people's homes to make them sing. For me this was the most satisfying tale.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1598978407i/30046895.png" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>It's a good book but you'll probably need something light and fun after reading it.<br><br>You can follow my reviews at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://reviewsbybarbsaffer.blogspot.com/">https://reviewsbybarbsaffer.blogspot....</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="6407405473658490566" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Forrest" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/217366-forrest"><img alt="Forrest" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1241306802p2/217366.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3476882?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Forrest" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Forrest" href="/user/show/217366-forrest">Forrest</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3476882" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8803186138898606127">Birds of America is a story collection by one of the most talented (but minimal) writers around, Lorrie Moore. The stories here are not big or grand or epic, but work simply as little one-act plays, exposing the inherent complexities and dramas in the everyday lives we all lead. <br/><br/>Moore's writing style is subtle, and laced with a fantastic sense of wit; witness, for example, her slight mocking of the health fad craze in the names she creates for juice bars; or her sly commentary about the misnomer</span>
  <span id="freeText8803186138898606127" style="display:none">Birds of America is a story collection by one of the most talented (but minimal) writers around, Lorrie Moore. The stories here are not big or grand or epic, but work simply as little one-act plays, exposing the inherent complexities and dramas in the everyday lives we all lead. <br /><br />Moore's writing style is subtle, and laced with a fantastic sense of wit; witness, for example, her slight mocking of the health fad craze in the names she creates for juice bars; or her sly commentary about the misnomer of "busy bee" in the story "Whatever You Want, Fine." Added to this wit is a keen sense of what it means to be on this earth and to interact with someone else (I'm being cliche here, because I'm no Lorrie Moore), to have an effect upon someone's life simply because you happen to walk a similar road together for awhile, and it is the way in which she explores this truth that gives her stories the weight they need to avoid being simple comic pieces. <br /><br />The three best stories, in my opinion, are "Four Calling Birds, Three French Hens," "People Like That Are The Only People Here," and "Which is More Than I Can Say About Some People." The first story deals with a woman who has a wonderfully loving husband and a great daughter, but can't get over the loss of her cat, and so decides to undergo therapy that guarentees to "cure her by Christmas or the last session is free!" The second deals with pediatric oncology in a harrowing and moving way, about the distance illness brings between people, and the last deals with a mother and daughter, on a road trip through Ireland, and the ways in which revelations of character don't end simply because you know someone for years and years. <br /><br />These are wonderful, wonderful stories, the kind that make you think about your own life, examine it in the ways the characters are examined here, that affirm realizations you yourself have come to or guide you toward ones you haven't, and I don't think you can ask for much more than that from a book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8803186138898606127" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10468770189696713743">I’m not a huge fan of short stories, and I feel like I was rather fooled by the cover of this book into tackling it. I have worked with the Whooping Crane reintroduction program here in Calgary, exercising young crane chicks, and I simply couldn’t resist the pretty cover with the Whooping Crane on it. Plus that alluring title (for a birder), <i>Birds of America</i>. How either the image or the title relate to the stories within remains a mystery to me.<br/><br/>Moore’s stories are rather bleak views of human rel</span>
  <span id="freeText10468770189696713743" style="display:none">I’m not a huge fan of short stories, and I feel like I was rather fooled by the cover of this book into tackling it. I have worked with the Whooping Crane reintroduction program here in Calgary, exercising young crane chicks, and I simply couldn’t resist the pretty cover with the Whooping Crane on it. Plus that alluring title (for a birder), <i>Birds of America</i>. How either the image or the title relate to the stories within remains a mystery to me.<br /><br />Moore’s stories are rather bleak views of human relationships—told from all kinds of angles but with similar disappointments to go round. As in this dinner party exchange:<br /><br /><blockquote> "The thing to remember about love affairs," says Simone, "is that they are all like having raccoons in your chimney."<br />"Oh, not the raccoon story," groans Cal.<br />"Yes! The raccoons!" cries Eugene.<br />I'm sawing at my duck.<br />"We have raccoons sometimes in our chimney," explains Simone.<br />"Hmmm," I say, not surprised.<br />"And once we tried to smoke them out. We lit a fire, knowing they were there, but we hoped that the smoke would cause them to scurry out the top and never come back. Instead, they caught on fire and came crashing down into our living room, all charred and in flames and running madly around until they<br />dropped dead." <br />Simone swallows some wine. "Love affairs are like that," she says. "They all are like that."</blockquote><br /><br />I don’t believe I’ve ever had a love affair which ended quite so spectacularly. Apparently, I am doing it wrong.<br /></span>

<div id="review_6490942" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6490942" />
    <a title="Sara Batkie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/334035-sara-batkie"><img alt="Sara Batkie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570761469p2/334035.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6490942?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara Batkie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara Batkie" href="/user/show/334035-sara-batkie">Sara Batkie</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/334035-sara-batkie?shelf=sweet-on-them">sweet-on-them</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6490942" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10121783186478171608">Maybe the most perfect short story collection I've read (that wasn't a "collected works" or "best of"). I understand the criticisms of "same-y" characters and too-witty dialogue, but frankly I don't care. Lorrie Moore can wrap me around her little finger any time. Kakutani's back blurb calls the book: "sad, funny, lyrical, and prickly" and that's probably the best way to describe her. She is awash in those kinds of contradictions, but it's what makes her stories a joy to read. You always end up </span>
  <span id="freeText10121783186478171608" style="display:none">Maybe the most perfect short story collection I've read (that wasn't a "collected works" or "best of"). I understand the criticisms of "same-y" characters and too-witty dialogue, but frankly I don't care. Lorrie Moore can wrap me around her little finger any time. Kakutani's back blurb calls the book: "sad, funny, lyrical, and prickly" and that's probably the best way to describe her. She is awash in those kinds of contradictions, but it's what makes her stories a joy to read. You always end up where you least expected to go but somehow in her absurd little world, it makes sense. There were times I laughed aloud: "He doesn't believe in shouting 'Fire' in a crowded movie theater, but he does believe in shouting 'Fie!' and has done it twice himself - both times at Forrest Gump" (132) and there were times I wept, and there were times her prose got something so right that I just sat there dumbfounded: "She knew she was saying good-bye to something important, which was not that bad, in a way, because it meant that at least you had said hello to it to begin with" (80). I'm sure I'll start imitating her now. Sigh.<br />I have a habit of highlighting my favorite stories in the contents as I go along. They were: "Which is More Than I Can Say About Some People", "Dance in America", "Charades", "What You Want to Do Fine", "People Like That Are the Only People Here" (probably the very best, if you only read one, read this), and "Terrific Mother". All the stories are well done and worth reading, though.<br />And psst, Ms. Moore, it's been almost ten years since this book came out. I demand more! (he he, sorry, had to)</span>
  <a data-text-id="10121783186478171608" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2896506372" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2896506372" />
    <a title="Sketchbook" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4708498-sketchbook"><img alt="Sketchbook" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1296491148p2/4708498.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2896506372?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sketchbook" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sketchbook" href="/user/show/4708498-sketchbook">Sketchbook</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2896506372" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17284780366253234141">Adrienne gets to accompany her husband, a research economist, to a posh artist-academic retreat where food and studios are provided while you pursue your writing or art with two dozen or so other guests. You must, however, dress for dinner. The seating is carefully arranged for "the cross-pollination of ideas." You might hear words like <i>Heideggerian</i> and <i>ideological</i> while munching <i>salsiccia alla griglia con spinaci, </i> but Adrienne has to gird herself when there's a discussion of peptides and rabbi</span>
  <span id="freeText17284780366253234141" style="display:none">Adrienne gets to accompany her husband, a research economist, to a posh artist-academic retreat where food and studios are provided while you pursue your writing or art with two dozen or so other guests. You must, however, dress for dinner. The seating is carefully arranged for "the cross-pollination of ideas." You might hear words like <i>Heideggerian</i> and <i>ideological</i> while munching <i>salsiccia alla griglia con spinaci, </i> but Adrienne has to gird herself when there's a discussion of peptides and rabbit tests or learning that lobsters have what's called a hemipenis. What the mischief is Adrienne doing there? "I married my husband," she asserts, "because I thought it would be a great way to meet guys."<br /><br />Ah, you've never spent a month or two at such courtesy colonies? Adrienne is at what I assume to be the rather hoity Bellagio Center (Rockefeller funded) on Lake Como in Italy. There are several such places, not so fancy, of course, in the northeast US, and, indeed, I did time at one some years ago, longing to return to the confusion of NYC where I can really find my own serenity -- without the distraction of other colonists, who all bored me to death and, unlike the Bellagio, where the food was forgettable. I skipped breakfast, which stopped long before I awoke at the crack of ten or eleven, and lunch came in a picnic basket discreetly delivered to your studio. When I heard the put-put car motor of Little Red Riding Hood I'd jump to my desk and start typing. But dinner, where you had to mingle and chat, was fairly unbearable, as Adrienne also discovered. "I don't believe in casual sex," she deadpans to a friend, "I believe in casual marriage." Then I met a new arrival, the novelist Iris Owens, aka Harriet Daimler, and we'd huddle together wondering which guests were screwing each other (there was a lot of <i>that)</i> and down vodkas from the bottle she conveniently toted, which helped digestion. <br /><br />Moore's story, "Terrific Mother," is Lorrie Moore at her very best. It's a laugh out loud sendup of such retreats or colonies, and I highly recommend to anyone with a conquering sense of humor. Adrienne lands at her retreat, you see, because she accidentally killed a friend's baby---. Ok, I stop here.<br /><br />Many of Moore's stories deal with some terrible illness or disability: cancer, cystic fibrosis, Down Syndrome or just plain blindness. Death hovers. But her sad-funny writing always prickles. Admittedly, her women are usually depressives or needy, but then dropping a baby on stone is not exactly a fender bender, or is it?</span>
  <a data-text-id="17284780366253234141" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2998964697" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer11482817705067768737">The stories in this book hit upon universal themes; loss, loneliness, love, finding acceptance, overcoming fears- in short: LIFE.<br/>There is so much sadness and heartbreak in these stories, but there is also humour. By the end of the book, I must admit I couldn’t recall one truly happy story.<br/>Lorrie Moore is a master of the short story. Each story felt complete. I found her to be very clever with her words.<br/><br/>“ Her mother had given her the name Agnes, believing that a good looking woman was even more s</span>
  <span id="freeText11482817705067768737" style="display:none">The stories in this book hit upon universal themes; loss, loneliness, love, finding acceptance, overcoming fears- in short: LIFE.<br />There is so much sadness and heartbreak in these stories, but there is also humour. By the end of the book, I must admit I couldn’t recall one truly happy story.<br />Lorrie Moore is a master of the short story. Each story felt complete. I found her to be very clever with her words.<br /><br />“ Her mother had given her the name Agnes, believing that a good looking woman was even more striking when her name was a homely one.”<br /><br />“ Perhaps, she thought, that was where affection began: in an unlikely phrase, in a moment of someone’s having unexpectedly but at last said the right thing.”<br /><br />My favourites in the collection were: Four Calling Birds, Three French Hens; Which is More than I Can Say; People Like That Are the Only People Here: Canonical Babbling in Peed Onk; and Charades, which is honestly the funniest story as well. ( Christmas family squabbling humour!)<br /><br />I recommend this book to anyone who enjoys short stories. Lorrie Moore is an excellent writer and will not disappoint you:)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11482817705067768737" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

The raccoons!" cries Eugene.<br/>I'm sawing at my duck.<br/>"We have raccoons sometimes in our chimney," explains<br/>Simone.<br/>"Hmmm," I say, not surprised.<br/>"And once we tried to smoke them out.   <span id="freeText8773075075763468739" style="display:none">"The thing to remember about love affairs," says Simone, "is that they are all like having raccoons in your chimney."<br />"Oh, not the raccoon story," groans Cal.<br />"Yes! The raccoons!" cries Eugene.<br />I'm sawing at my duck.<br />"We have raccoons sometimes in our chimney," explains<br />Simone.<br />"Hmmm," I say, not surprised.<br />"And once we tried to smoke them out. <div id="review_1241678378" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1241678378" />
    <a title="Andrew Hicks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29779942-andrew-hicks"><img alt="Andrew Hicks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395286088p2/29779942.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1241678378?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew Hicks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew Hicks" href="/user/show/29779942-andrew-hicks">Andrew Hicks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1241678378" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5174396143259621518">To celebrate my one-year anniversary of joining Goodreads, I've decided to read and review one 5-star book from each of the five favorite new reading buddies I've met on GR. First up is <i>
  <b>Birds of America</b>
</i>, a collection of 12 short stories from Lorrie Moore. It's a favorite book of my new pal <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/1899836-snotchocheez' rel='nofollow noopener'>Snotchocheez</a>, a kind, wise, supportive, all-around cool guy.<br/><br/>Now, in 1998, when <i>
  <b>BOA</b>
</i> was released, I was 20. I was a junior in college at Mizzou. I was fed up for life with the academic approach to analy</span>
  <span id="freeText5174396143259621518" style="display:none">To celebrate my one-year anniversary of joining Goodreads, I've decided to read and review one 5-star book from each of the five favorite new reading buddies I've met on GR. First up is <i>
  <b>Birds of America</b>
</i>, a collection of 12 short stories from Lorrie Moore. It's a favorite book of my new pal <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/1899836-snotchocheez" rel="nofollow noopener">Snotchocheez</a>, a kind, wise, supportive, all-around cool guy.<br /><br />Now, in 1998, when <i>
  <b>BOA</b>
</i> was released, I was 20. I was a junior in college at Mizzou. I was fed up for life with the academic approach to analyzing literature. Call me simple (and do it slowly, with exaggerated head nods, to increase my chances of understanding you), but I like to read for enjoyment. My holy trinity is plot, character and voice, and that's all I need. I don't care about symbolism, and I don't want to spend an hour dissecting the deep meaning of a single descriptive paragraph. <br /><br /><i>
  <b>BOA</b>
</i> was a double whammy - a lofty, cerebral book designed to be cherished and studied by intellectuals that is itself densely populated with professors, writers and miscellaneous academic nimrods. It views them with the correct amount of detached irony while still being of the same mind, which to me was a blessing and a curse. <br /><br />I'm glad I read <i>
  <b>BOA</b>
</i>, and on any given page there were phrases and observations to love, with abundant sparkling wit applied toward a broad spectrum of human experiences. But this book was never a close friend or a page-turner. Its place was more in the lecture hall of my soul than the dorm room or even the dining hall. (I love the dining hall.)<br /><br />This is a collection of twelve short stories, all but one of them previously published in the <i>
  <b>New Yorker, Harper's, The Paris Review</b>
</i>, et al. The protagonists of most of these stories are restless, unsatisfied women in their thirties who distract themselves with devoted lesser men. Collectively, most of these characters are interchangeable. <br /><br />Which made me think, when you have a bunch of same-ish stories that feature same-ish protagonists, why not composite them into a single novel? But then I thought, <i>Well, because it's easier to just write a story here, a story there, get two bucks a word to publish them in a lit mag, then re-edit and compile them into a single book, get a fat check from Random House, do a nice little public-appearance tour to support it, then go back to your day job as tenured professor of English at the University of Wisconsin-Madison, where you can assign your own book to your students as required reading and then spend a semester's worth of classes talking about how good it is using nothing but Big Words.</i> Geez, I'm cynical.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>BOA</b>
</i> gains momentum as it goes, though. The first real break from the same-ish protagonist formula is like four stories from the end, with a vacationing gay couple where one is blind and the other is actually straight, or thought he was. You'll see seriously sick little kids (like, nearing death) pop up here and there, culminating in "People Like That Are the Only People Here," the most heart-wrenching story in the book. That story tunnels further into the human psyche than I could even attempt, and has some bitter belly laughs to boot.<br /><br />The last story, "Wonderful Mother," begins with its protagonist being handed a baby at a family-and-friends function, losing her balance and fatally dropping the baby on its head. So let me point out - this is the kind of premise the short-story format is perfect for. Do I want to be stuck with an accidental baby-killer protagonist for 350 pages? Nope. Will I spend 40 pages full-on engaged while she marries some professor just because he insists on it, then takes her to this nightmarish European academic retreat? You bet I will.<br /><br />By the way, I'm 37, I have a bachelor's degree, I'm a server in a fine-dining restaurant, I read a lot of young-adult fiction and celebrity memoirs, and my chances of going to grad school are still hovering steady at about 18%.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5174396143259621518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6171354063139648776" style="display:none">She's an excellent story writer - this is her best I think. I treasure my signed copy! <br /><br />just come across some notes on this from a 1998 notebook: <br />her stuff can hit like a brick round the head. She recounts bruising, tiresome relationships fearlessly, picking the right moments out. Bitterness, the brutality of what we can think and feel, and how we can't forgive ourselves. In 'Real Estate' a death from lung cancer, intertwined with a story of a burglar who makes his victims sing and writes down the words, but can't recall the tunes when he gets home. The singing night, the spaceship like a set of lit teeth in the sky; the page and a half of Ha's! In Peed Onk a community of pain the mother doesn't want to join, but can't help. And the last one about a woman who accidentally kills a baby, marries an academic who is patient, awkward with her, takes her to Italy for a month with other academics. Great stuff.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer20213810" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14179697562823828177">Beautiful stories, obviously. This is also the book that everyone says YOU MUST READ THIS. So, of course I am resistant to it. I did like it, but I also felt like each story hit a similar note...which is probably a good thing for a collection, but is a bad thing for a reader. All of her stories (in here, nowhere else) have this feeling of spending a day in a musty house to me...not sure if that makes sense.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307474964" href="/book/show/7076796-birds-of-america">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2499261-rachel?shelf=lady-lit">lady-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2499261-rachel?shelf=dusty-bookshelf">dusty-bookshelf</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2363811070" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8718181220620555447">I <i>loved</i> three of the stories in this collection: "Charades," about familial aggressions played out over a holiday game, "What You Want to Do Fine," about the breakdown of a romance between two men on endless road trip vacations across the US, and "Four Calling Birds, Three French Hens," about a married mother mourning her dead cat - which, despite sounding like it would mostly be sad, is also so deadpan funny that I laughed until I cried at one point. <br/><br/>I would say that I enjoyed reading about nin</span>
  <span id="freeText8718181220620555447" style="display:none">I <i>loved</i> three of the stories in this collection: "Charades," about familial aggressions played out over a holiday game, "What You Want to Do Fine," about the breakdown of a romance between two men on endless road trip vacations across the US, and "Four Calling Birds, Three French Hens," about a married mother mourning her dead cat - which, despite sounding like it would mostly be sad, is also so deadpan funny that I laughed until I cried at one point. <br /><br />I would say that I enjoyed reading about nine out of twelve stories, which is pretty good for a bigger story collection. My feeling about <i>Birds of America</i> reflects my feeling about Lorrie Moore's work in general. Mostly, it strikes me as funny and spot-on in terms of human oddness and and vulnerabilities, but sometimes her writing crosses over into too much quirk (contrivance?) to be relatable.<br /><br />Note: I have not read this two times, I have read it once. Goodreads will not let me change it back from read twice. WTF, Goodreads.</span>

<div id="review_207426290" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/207426290" />
    <a title="Antonia Crane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1016009-antonia-crane"><img alt="Antonia Crane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1396333490p2/1016009.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/207426290?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antonia Crane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antonia Crane" href="/user/show/1016009-antonia-crane">Antonia Crane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer207426290" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9998021901382156989">Wildly ambitious and witty and brutal, Moore's stories are ambitious as they are smart. Rob Roberge referred to her stories at Antioch with fondness, and I was required to read "Anagrams" one project period, but I didn't fall in love with her like I did with her collection of short stories: "Birds of America." In "What You Want to Do Fine," she tackles a difficult love between men, war, the draft, lost children, AIDS, blindness and the parade of bones in cemetery visits. She personalizes the hop</span>
  <span id="freeText9998021901382156989" style="display:none">Wildly ambitious and witty and brutal, Moore's stories are ambitious as they are smart. Rob Roberge referred to her stories at Antioch with fondness, and I was required to read "Anagrams" one project period, but I didn't fall in love with her like I did with her collection of short stories: "Birds of America." In "What You Want to Do Fine," she tackles a difficult love between men, war, the draft, lost children, AIDS, blindness and the parade of bones in cemetery visits. She personalizes the hopeless boredom and gentleness of real intimacy between two people and the question that echoes is what is it to love and know someone? In "Real Estate" she smashed the narrative structure with her 2.5 pages of "Ha!" as a response to her husbands multiple affairs that happen in Spring. She shows where rage goes when it folds one's insides like origami. She does this with her witty genius and tricky twists of fate with other characters who act out a similar rage. But, the story that left the deepest imprint was "People Like That Are the Only People Here" which did loops around illness and the type of loss that happens, how it shreds relationships thread by thread and how when it's over, it's hollow survivors never want to see those people again, that sadness again. Ever. At the same time, the only thing survivors can relate to is grief and tragedy, like baby birds bonding in desperation. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9998021901382156989" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2183178739" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2183178739" />
    <a title="Riva Sciuto" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2660322-riva-sciuto"><img alt="Riva Sciuto" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583534803p2/2660322.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2183178739?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Riva Sciuto" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Riva Sciuto" href="/user/show/2660322-riva-sciuto">Riva Sciuto</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2660322-riva-sciuto?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2660322-riva-sciuto?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2183178739" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14059843241145143602">"Life: what an absurd little story it always made." <br/><br/>***<br/><br/>This short story collection reveals exactly why Lorrie Moore is one of the most revered authors of our time. The twelve stories that comprise 'Birds of America' are hilariously funny, painfully sad, and deeply human. "Oh, the rich torment that was life," she writes in "Real Estate." Perhaps what I love most about her stories are the characters that bring them to life: they are real people, full of fears and flaws and imperfections. They reve</span>
  <span id="freeText14059843241145143602" style="display:none">"Life: what an absurd little story it always made." <br /><br />***<br /><br />This short story collection reveals exactly why Lorrie Moore is one of the most revered authors of our time. The twelve stories that comprise 'Birds of America' are hilariously funny, painfully sad, and deeply human. "Oh, the rich torment that was life," she writes in "Real Estate." Perhaps what I love most about her stories are the characters that bring them to life: they are real people, full of fears and flaws and imperfections. They reveal the complexities that make us all human: our longing to find meaning in our lives; our desire for human connection; and the sad but encouraging universality of our losses. <br /><br />In Moore's stories, we meet the vulnerable and the lonely and the grief-stricken and the ambitious. In "Willing," we meet "a man held hostage by the anxious cast of his dream" and a woman who "was unequal to anyone's wistfulness. She had made too little of her life. Its loneliness shamed her like a crime." In "Community Life," we meet the mysterious Olena, who "wished to start over again, to be someone living coltishly in the world, not someone hidden away, behind books, with a carefully learned voice and a sad past." In "Beautiful Grade," we meet Bill, whose "own sadness ... slosh about in his life in a low-key way, formless and self-consuming." In "What You Want To Do Fine," we are introduced to Quilty, whose blindness never stops him from wanting to see the world around him.<br /><br />These stories are rich with both humor and sadness, no doubt the sign of remarkable writing. Moore has a unique ability to capture with words the depths of human suffering and the breadth of human emotions in a way I've never quite seen before. More than anything, she reminds us that a full life is full of beauty and suffering, of darkness and joy. In "Real Estate," Moore writes, "She never knew anymore what was good life and what was bad, what was desirable matter and what was antimatter, what was the thing itself and what was the death of the thing: one mimicked the other, and she resented the work of having to distinguish." In the book's final story, "Terrific Mother," Moore highlights the complexity and ambiguity of life: "...In this temporary dissolve, seeing death and birth, seeing the beginning and then the end, how they were the same quiet black, same nothing ever after: everyone's life appeared in the world like a dark movie in a room. First dark, then light, then dark again. But it was all staggered, so that somewhere there was always light." <br /><br />I'll end with my favorite passage from this book, which comes from "Which Is More Than I Can Say About Some People": "Abby began to think that all the beauty and ugliness and turbulence one found scattered through nature, one could also find in people themselves, all collected there, all together in a single place. No matter what terror or loveliness the earth could produce -- winds, seas -- a person could produce the same, lived with the same, lived with all that mixed-up nature swirling inside, every bit. There was nothing as complex in the world -- no flower or stone -- as a single hello from a human being." <br /><br />Five stars for this beautiful and unforgettable collection.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14059843241145143602" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Billy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/390579-billy"><img alt="Billy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1326581593p2/390579.jpg" /></a>

        <a title="Billy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Billy" href="/user/show/390579-billy">Billy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307474964" href="/book/show/7076796-birds-of-america">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer116903296" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14595371305428503587"><p>First of all, with Lorrie Moore: Oh, the puns!</p><br/><p>Second of all, she doesn't really do men. There's one story in here that takes a male character's perspective, and it's one of the weakest. It's a good faith effort, but the shortcoming seems to stem from a genuine befuddlement as to how men might dwell in themselves, how they might carry themselves from one moment to the next. That's not to say she is <i>limited</i> as writing from a specifically gendered perspective, or that she reads like a feminine "typ</p></span>
  <span id="freeText14595371305428503587" style="display:none"><p>First of all, with Lorrie Moore: Oh, the puns!</p><br /><p>Second of all, she doesn't really do men. There's one story in here that takes a male character's perspective, and it's one of the weakest. It's a good faith effort, but the shortcoming seems to stem from a genuine befuddlement as to how men might dwell in themselves, how they might carry themselves from one moment to the next. That's not to say she is <i>limited</i> as writing from a specifically gendered perspective, or that she reads like a feminine "type"; I don't see her able to write with sympathy from the point of view of plenty of women characters. But her signature narrative voice is so personal (and clear, the way you always know which one is the "Woody Allen character" in his movies, no matter who's playing it), built on earned experience and observation, that it isn't as compelling when she briefly inhabits a different perspective, like a tourist climbing up a bell tower to take in the vista for ten minutes before turning back, with a way of seeing that is keenly sensitive but still superficial, transient. </p> <br /><p>Now that that's off my chest, let me thirdly say that this collection has put Lorrie Moore in the top two or three of my favorite authors. When describing literature you like, it's easy to reach for superlatives ("the most insightful prose and thoughtful character exploration"; "never been a more sensitive accounting of the rhythms of emotion") in an attempt to sell your friends on it in the hope that they will read the same thing and feel how you feel when you were in the midst of it so that your sense of elevation, of light, of indigestion will be <i>confirmed</i> in another person, meaning that you were not after all drifting alone when you were moved, and that what you felt was real, at least in the feeling of it. But her writing, at its best, seems indifferent to where it ranks on the scales of vividness, or poignancy, or wit. She's not looking for the truth - is there any more superlative concept? She's not trying to wow you with her language (her inability to resist said puns being key evidence of that). Although she is mightily skilled in descriptive imagery, inspiration and judgment on opposite ends of the seesaw (and the former a bit heavier), the craftsmanship is not displayed for its own gaudy sake, but is the fruit of careful looking at what it means to be human. She's interested in the experience of the personal which - somehow, unexpectedly, in our never-more-self-obsessed time, is missed, either because it has fallen into another dimension, or perhaps because it doesn't exist at all. As one of her characters mouths back at "the Lorrie Moore character" at one point, "this is the kind of thing that fiction is: it's the unlivable life, the strange room tacked onto the house, the extra moon that is circling the earth unbeknownst to science." Perhaps it's enough to leave it at this: Lorrie Moore makes me want to read more and makes me daydream about writing myself someday, or never; either way, as long as I think about it.</p> <br /><p>Lastly, there's the crush. There's an episode of <i>Entourage</i> in which a woman meets and goes to bed with Vinnie Chase and, the next morning, explains to him, after his surprise at learning that she is engaged and imminently to be married, that she and her fiancé have an understanding that, given the opportunity, they are allowed to sleep with specific celebrities on a pre-defined and pre-disclosed list; Vince is on the list. Were C and I to have a similar arrangement, I would be tempted to include Ms. Moore. And maybe not really for the soggy awkwardness of a surely underwhelming (from her perspective) physical encounter, but at least for the chance to buy her a drink, tell a few jokes, maybe offer her a shot of wry - and be flirted back at for a half hour or so. I saw a comment on a youtube clip of a talk she was giving that she "makes disdain beautiful." Maybe it's just the rarified glamour of being a commercially successful art fiction writer who's also fetching, but I have to agree - wise, mordant, so wonderfully Irish. Sigh.</p> </span>
  <a data-text-id="14595371305428503587" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_139323810" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/139323810" />
    <a title="Jeff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1313994-jeff"><img alt="Jeff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1333515487p2/1313994.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/139323810?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeff" href="/user/show/1313994-jeff">Jeff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer139323810" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1502610866626322040">The trouble I have with most short story books is that I tend to forget what the first story was about by the time I get to the last one. In lieu of this trend wherein I do not think I inhabit my oubliette alone, most short story writers attempt to create vignettes (heavy on the French today) that are tiredly unique to each other. Moore, I think, attempts solidarity by thematically and tonally creating stories that are strikingly similar. Dare I say that each narrator has the same Mooreish wit a</span>
  <span id="freeText1502610866626322040" style="display:none">The trouble I have with most short story books is that I tend to forget what the first story was about by the time I get to the last one. In lieu of this trend wherein I do not think I inhabit my oubliette alone, most short story writers attempt to create vignettes (heavy on the French today) that are tiredly unique to each other. Moore, I think, attempts solidarity by thematically and tonally creating stories that are strikingly similar. Dare I say that each narrator has the same Mooreish wit an analyst could presume is a solid embodiment of Moore's own cynical wit and perspective?<br /><br />Thematically, there are golden threads that link the concepts of place or where one is from, relationships and tired fidelity, child death (heavy on the gut-Wrench today), academia's ivory toilet, and American Diaspora who live at home. America is so large, and filled with so many identities, that to be an American means to sometimes be disjointed from one's own identity. It's the willing to please but not knowing one's own pleasure ideology that pervades Americana, and Moore gets it. She gets it she gets it she gets it, by God.<br /><br />She's brilliant and pretty; her prose that sort of beautiful that doesn't hide its shy brilliance. It's like that blond in high school us bookish kids wished was a little smarter, to justify our lust.<br /><br />But really, it was just the hormones.<br /><br />You may not remember which story was what, but the book that is realized in their telling is one for the shelf, not a damn Kindle (tm).<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1502610866626322040" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_7218520" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/7218520" />
    <a title="Stephanie Sun" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/118030-stephanie-sun"><img alt="Stephanie Sun" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589823798p2/118030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/7218520?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie Sun" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie Sun" href="/user/show/118030-stephanie-sun">Stephanie Sun</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/118030-stephanie-sun?shelf=dead-tree">dead-tree</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/118030-stephanie-sun?shelf=clinton">clinton</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/118030-stephanie-sun?shelf=bechdel-test">bechdel-test</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer7218520" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2553998069704651508"><blockquote>
  <i>“There are the notes. Now where is the money?”</i>
</blockquote><br/>The short story is no longer the commercial storytelling medium that it was in its heydey, but the copyright page of <b>Birds of America</b> is strong evidence that the short story system can still work incredibly well as an incubator of literary talent and new work. The publisher writes:<br/><blockquote>Eleven of these stories were originally published in slightly different form in the following: Elle: ‘Agnes of Iowa’; Harper’s: ‘What You Want to Do Fine’ (originally t</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText2553998069704651508" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“There are the notes. Now where is the money?”</i>
</blockquote><br />The short story is no longer the commercial storytelling medium that it was in its heydey, but the copyright page of <b>Birds of America</b> is strong evidence that the short story system can still work incredibly well as an incubator of literary talent and new work. The publisher writes:<br /><blockquote>Eleven of these stories were originally published in slightly different form in the following: Elle: ‘Agnes of Iowa’; Harper’s: ‘What You Want to Do Fine’ (originally titled ‘Lucky Ducks’); The New York Times: ‘Four Calling Birds, Three French Hens’ (originally titled ‘If Only Bert Were Here’); The New Yorker: ‘Beautiful Grade,’ ‘Charades,’ ‘Community Life,’ ‘Dance in America,’ ‘People Like That Are the Only People Here,’ ‘Which Is More Than I Can Say About Some People,’ and ‘Willing’; The Paris Review: ‘Terrific Mother’<br /></blockquote><br />Widely considered Moore’s strongest book to date, <b>Birds of America</b> benefited from Moore’s collaborations not just with her Knopf editor Victoria Wilson, but with an additional eight talented periodical editors, including George Plimpton and Bill Buford.<br /><br />(The one story in <b>Birds of America</b> not listed above, “Real Estate,” was published in <b>The New Yorker</b> in August 1998, as part of the book’s public roll-out.)<br /><br />These collaborations seem to have brought a welcome discipline to Moore’s work, deepening the end result. What was once “merely” clever has become wise, and what was merely striking has become clear and true.<br /><br />Dave Eggers in <a href="http://www.salon.com/1998/10/02/sneaks_166/" rel="nofollow noopener">his review in Salon</a> summed up Moore’s concerns best, somehow capturing both the darkness and tongue-in-cheek humor of her vision in a single bulleted list:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>Moore’s stories are about these things:<br />• Longing<br />• Suffering<br />• People mistakenly dropping babies on their head in such a way that the baby dies<br />• Depression, or at least life’s way of sort of stalling at middle age<br />• Depression, or at least life’s way of sort of stalling during that period just before middle age<br />• Depression, or at least life’s way of stalling at any age at all, really<br />• Marriages and affairs that are hopeless but serviceable, like a scratchy, Army-issue blanket<br />• Creature comforts in the face of unfaceable pathos<br />• Lives that would warrant suicide if the owner could find the inspiration<br />• Friends who make you laugh<br />• Easy puns<br />• At least one person per story with cancer<br />• Perhaps a child with cancer, too</i>
</blockquote><br />There are other writers who successfully tackle similar material, are funny, and are also better (or at least more consistently good) than Moore at other fundamentals like character development, social commentary, and plot. As part of the argument for reading <b>Birds of America</b>—its contemporary setting—fades with time, one is forced to consider the overall value of the offering here. Birds was a standout book of 1998, sure, but should this be included among the stories that students of the future use to access Clinton-era America in the way that students of today access the Gilded Age via Fitzgerald and Waugh?<br /><br />It doesn’t take much time browsing through the book with this question in mind to surface ample evidence that the correct answer is a resounding ‘Yes.’<br /><br />Take the subtitle of what is perhaps Moore’s most iconic work, the Bill Buford-edited “People Like That Are the Only People Here”: CANONICAL BABBLING IN PEED ONK.<br /><br />The urination- and pig-sound-looking “Peed Onk” is, we find out, what people embedded in the world of Pediatric Oncology call Pediatric Oncology because, presumably, they must say the words that mean “the treatment and study of tumors in children” so often that they have shortened the phrase for convenience and also have become numb to it. Moore, by including it in its most juvenile and ridiculous incarnation in the subtitle and then revealing its horror halfway through her story, very effectively calls attention to the increased jargonization of everyday American language, and by extension the increased commodification and conformity of everyday American life.<br /><br />Peed Onk, like much of modern progress, is a double-eged sword, something that Moore explores explicitly in the coda to “People Like That,” whose tacked-on feeling is deceptive. Indeed, the coda, with its perplexingly anti-social message, is perhaps the point of the story. The character known as The Husband asks the character known as The Mother what she thinks about the other brave Peed Onk parents as they leave Peed Onk with child in remission in tow:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>“‘Don’t you feel consoled, knowing we’re all in the same boat, that we’re all in this together?’”</i>
</blockquote><br />Surprising The Husband and the reader, The Mother replies in the negative:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>“‘Let’s make our own way,’ ... ‘and not in this boat.’”</i>
</blockquote><br />Conformity (even in the context of the courageous endurance to be found at cancer bedsides) equals death. That’s as fierce of a rallying cry for the survival of iconoclasm, and iconoclasm as survival, as anything Frank Sinatra crooned.<br /><br />Lorrie Moore: American as a flipped bird. Who knew?<br /><br />-<br /><br />PREVIOUSLY:<br /><br />These stories shouldn't work. They are riddled with twitchy wordplay and characters without clear motivations, who seem to revel in their own mediocrity or other middleness (middle age, middle America).<br /><br />My favorite story in this collection is about a childless woman who accidentally kills her friend's baby, accepts her boyfriend's spontaneous marriage proposal in order to accompany him to an academic retreat in Italy, and then gets lots of massages. And it is somehow hilarious, brilliant, romantic, heartwarming, and a page-turning read.<br /><br />I can't promise that it's everyone's cup of tea, and I know it isn't, but I've found plenty of re-read value in this book, as well as wisdom about relationships and enduring crisis. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2553998069704651508" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_288522697" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/288522697" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/82840-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1465057364p2/82840.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/288522697?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/82840-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer288522697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11471896816477316102">I finally gave up reading this book yesterday after getting about 2/3 of the way through it. It is always so hard for me to give up a book -- my ego gets entangled in the idea that I MUST finish reading it... but I realized that I was wasting time with this book that was depressing me instead of filling my life with books that engage and inspire me. Moore's writing is sometimes right on in her descriptions of people. For example, in one story, the sentence "She freezes hams" perfectly encapsulat</span>
  <span id="freeText11471896816477316102" style="display:none">I finally gave up reading this book yesterday after getting about 2/3 of the way through it. It is always so hard for me to give up a book -- my ego gets entangled in the idea that I MUST finish reading it... but I realized that I was wasting time with this book that was depressing me instead of filling my life with books that engage and inspire me. Moore's writing is sometimes right on in her descriptions of people. For example, in one story, the sentence "She freezes hams" perfectly encapsulates the stock figure of a generic and somewhat dull sister-in-law. But that's just the problem. Too many of these characters are stock characters that I feel like I've seen before in other short stories about people in their middle lives who are unfulfilled for one reason or another. It seemed to me like many of these stories were about characters either finally feeling the depth of their lack of fulfillment or grief or fear or finding some small glimpse of hope in the middle of the lack of fulfillment. <br /><br />There were many passages that I liked, because they were sharp and originally turned with clever collocations of words. But then I got tired of that. Reading this book made me long for a good, winding Victorian novel where the goal is not to invent some fantastically clever new arrangement of words to make the reader go "Wow!" but to craft a good story with characters that we fall in love with and feel what they feel. I feel like this kind of writing gets a lot of praise in MFA circles and cutting edge literary circles. But this kind of writing feels like wallowing. I like books where characters grow and change, and I feel like I've learned something about life or about another way of living somewhere else in the world or about human nature. I didn't learn anything from this book except that humans are capable of doing a great deal of wallowing and of creating a lot of their own misery. Right now, I'm adding this book to the stack that I plan to sell to my local used book store, and I'm hoping that I get some money for it that I can use to buy books that I can really dive into. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11471896816477316102" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_97084061" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/97084061" />
    <a title="Lauren" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1856914-lauren"><img alt="Lauren" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/97084061?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren" href="/user/show/1856914-lauren">Lauren</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer97084061" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1625960523968160534">I don't get Loorie Moore. I read her novel A gate at the whatever...the first thing that I read by her. I'd heard it lauded as one of the greatest books of the year, which it was not. So I decided that I would try at one of her more famous books of short stories. I heard about how great it was, how it was a must read when it comes to the form. I hated it almost as much as the novel. Her characters are dispassionate, the stories about pretty much the same thing. It's a trying book to get through.</span>
  <span id="freeText1625960523968160534" style="display:none">I don't get Loorie Moore. I read her novel A gate at the whatever...the first thing that I read by her. I'd heard it lauded as one of the greatest books of the year, which it was not. So I decided that I would try at one of her more famous books of short stories. I heard about how great it was, how it was a must read when it comes to the form. I hated it almost as much as the novel. Her characters are dispassionate, the stories about pretty much the same thing. It's a trying book to get through. The only exception is the story "People like that are the only people here," which is in third person, but is closer to a first person narrative. There is actually feeling in the text (perhaps because it's basis stems from a personal experience that she had). But come on. She rants against academia and "the circle jerk" of academics and then has to put on a display of her intellectual capacity. In the abovementioned story she lists the names of the children in the cancer ward the last mentioned, Mort and Tod. I speak German and understand the French reference and I thougt well that was clever until she put in parenthesis (Mort and Tod!) Sigh. Why point out how clever you are. You made up the names. It's fiction. Look I named the characters after death in two different languages other than English. Why not a wink, itstead of a self-congratulations.<br /><br />She had some clever ideas...the robber who asks people to sing because he does not know one song. Although music pervades society, I still appreciate the absurdity of the character not knowing a song and then scribbling the lyrics down like mad while he is robbing people. In the same story, I also like the fact that the main character and her friend take up shooting practice, but use pieces that are relics of the past. But overall, I was bored. At least I wasn't angry like when I finished the novel. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1625960523968160534" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_62734997" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/62734997" />
    <a title="Saxon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/217922-saxon"><img alt="Saxon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1265440874p2/217922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/62734997?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Saxon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Saxon" href="/user/show/217922-saxon">Saxon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer62734997" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17586069363948738049">Lorrie Moore has really only one story. It usually has something to do with a mild tragedy back-dropped by a modern life that is so cluttered and unexplainable that no one really can see or pinpoint what the fuss is all about. As she works through each story, the main character(usually a woman) is slightly neurotic, says (or thinks) colorless, off-handed things that seem entirely inappropriate considering the circumstances and yet, seem entirely true to life as well. And, of course, like life (a</span>
  <span id="freeText17586069363948738049" style="display:none">Lorrie Moore has really only one story. It usually has something to do with a mild tragedy back-dropped by a modern life that is so cluttered and unexplainable that no one really can see or pinpoint what the fuss is all about. As she works through each story, the main character(usually a woman) is slightly neurotic, says (or thinks) colorless, off-handed things that seem entirely inappropriate considering the circumstances and yet, seem entirely true to life as well. And, of course, like life (and post-modernism) there really isn't ever resolution let alone a clear understanding of what was trying to be resolved. <br /><br />Admittedly, it doesn't sound all too fascinating and while she is formulaic in both plot and context, she pulls if off. Moore balances the tragically heartbreaking and the ironically comedic quite well and she does modern dialogue better than any writer out there (I really wish she would have written a screenplay at some point). You have definitely read stories like this before--they appear all the time in The New Yorker, The Atlantic or This American Life by east-coast educated writer with hipster cred--and while I tend to dislike and become extremely annoyed with such stories, Birds of America is, deservedly, one of a few exceptions. Despite being published almost 20 years ago, Birds of America hasn't aged much and still holds up against similar popular fiction of today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17586069363948738049" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_675945668" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/675945668" />
    <a title="William" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8744761-william"><img alt="William" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/675945668?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William" href="/user/show/8744761-william">William</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8744761-william?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer675945668" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4733766025265607936">Reading the first few stories, I liked this book, but by the end I loved it. Moore has a quirky writing style (and I mean that in a very positive sense) which can combine wit, deep emotion and a vague sadness in a single paragraph.<br/><br/>There is a sense of Raymond Carver here and there in the bleakness of the largely (entirely?) Midwestern settings, but also something reminiscent of the zaniness of Donald Barthelme. There is almost always a sense of people reaching to connect and not quite making it, </span>
  <span id="freeText4733766025265607936" style="display:none">Reading the first few stories, I liked this book, but by the end I loved it. Moore has a quirky writing style (and I mean that in a very positive sense) which can combine wit, deep emotion and a vague sadness in a single paragraph.<br /><br />There is a sense of Raymond Carver here and there in the bleakness of the largely (entirely?) Midwestern settings, but also something reminiscent of the zaniness of Donald Barthelme. There is almost always a sense of people reaching to connect and not quite making it, but this unrequitedness somehow never leaves the situation as seeming hopeless.<br /><br />All the human emotions I can think of appear among these stories. I also get the sense that laughter is sometimes all we have when faced with the impossible or unknowable. These stories occasionally made me laugh out loud, and often made me feel deeply.<br /><br />Finally, I am impressed with how different each story is from all the others. Two examples, of stories which somehow stay with me especially vibrantly: "What You Want to Do Is Fine," about two gay guys on a road trip, and "People Like That Are the Only People Here," about a couple with their baby in a pediatric cancer ward. This collection of stories is amazingly creative and distinctive, and the writing is both memorable and compelling.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4733766025265607936" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
